Conditional and Categorical Syllogism
Classification of syllogism and conditional inference rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main types of syllogism—conditional (istithna'i) and categorical (iqtirani)—and the valid inference patterns in conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
Syllogism is either conditional or categorical; each has distinct rules for inference.

Imam Razi begins by dividing syllogism (qiyas) into two fundamental types. The conditional syllogism involves a conditional premise and a categorical premise that affirms or negates one part. The categorical syllogism requires a common term. This lesson focuses on the definitions and the inference patterns in conjunctive and disjunctive conditional syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
Read the definition of conditional and categorical syllogism. Note that the conditional is composed of a conditional premise and a categorical premise that is either the same as one part of the conditional or its contradictory.
2
Examine the conjunctive conditional
In a conjunctive conditional (muttasila), the consequent may be more general than the antecedent. The valid inference is from the absence of the entailing (malzum) to the absence of the necessary (lazim), and from the absence of the necessary to the absence of the entailing? Actually, read carefully: the text lists what is inferred and what is not inferred.
The text says: 'يستدل من غير الملزوم ومن غير اللازم ولا يستدل من وجود اللازم ولا من عدم الملزوم' – we infer from non-existence of entailing and from non-existence of necessary, but not from existence of necessary nor from non-existence of entailing? This is a technical point.
3
Examine the disjunctive conditional
In a disjunctive conditional (munfasila), affirming one disjunct implies the negation of the other, and vice versa, producing four conclusions.
4
Summarize the inference rules
Condense the productive patterns: in conjunctive, certain inferences are valid; in disjunctive, all four forms are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القياس بالمعنى الأول ينقسم إلى استثنائي واقتراني، فالاستثنائي ما تركّب من مقدمتين: إحداهما شرطية والأخرى مقرونة بالسكن [؟] وتكون عين أحد طرفي الشرطية أو نقيضها، والاقتراني هو الذي لا يكون كذلك. والاستثنائي ينقسم إلى متصلة ومنفصلة، ففي المتصلة يحتمل أن يكون التالي وهو اللازم أعم من المقدم الذي هو الملزوم كوجود العلم ووجود الحياة، فيستدل من غير الملزوم ومن غير اللازم ولا يستدل من وجود اللازم ولا من عدم الملزوم. وقد أورد ذكر المنتج منها وغير المنتج في كلمات قليلة. وفي المنفصلة يستدل بعين كل واحد على نقيض الآخر وبالعكس فينتج أربع نتائج. وأما الاقتراني فلابد في مقدمتيه من جزء مشترك بينهما ومن جزء خاص لكل واحدة منهما فيتناسبا، وإذا التقى المشترك ثبت الحكم المطلوب بين المقدمتين وهو النتيجة. وينقسم إلى أربعة أقسام: محكوما به في إحداهما محكوما عليه في الأخرى، وإما محكوما به فيهما، وإما محكوما عليه فيهما. والأول ينقسم إلى ما يكون المشترك محكوما به في المقدمة التي يكون التالي من جزئها محكوما عليه في النتيجة ويسمى شكلا أولاً، وإما أن يكون بعكس ذلك ويسمى شكلا رابعا، ولا يورد في أكثر الكتب لبعده عن الطبع. وأما إذا كان المشترك محكوما به فيهما فيسمى شكلا ثانيا، وإذا كان محكوما عليه فيهما يسمى شكلا ثالثا. والعمدة هو الأول وينتج منه أربعة أضرب، لأن المقدمة التي تشتمل على المحكوم عليه في النتيجة يجب أن تكون موجبة كلية أو جزئية، والأخرى يجب أن تكون كلية موجبة أو سالبة، والنتائج أربع: موجبة كلية، موجبة جزئية، سالبة كلية، سالبة جزئية. وقد أورد ذلك في ألفاظ قليلة في غاية الإيجاز. والثاني أورده على سبيل الاختصار، والمنتجة
Then the syllogism in the first sense divides into conditional (istithna'i) and categorical (iqtirani). The conditional is that which is composed of two premises: one of them is conditional and the other is connected with al-sakan [?] and is either the same as one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory. The categorical is that which is not so. The conditional divides into conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). In the conjunctive, it is possible that the consequent (tali), which is the necessary (lazim), is more general than the antecedent (muqaddam), which is the entailing (malzum), such as the existence of knowledge and the existence of life. Thus one infers from the absence of the entailing and from the absence of the necessary, but one does not infer from the existence of the necessary nor from the absence of the entailing. He mentioned the productive and unproductive among them in brief words. In the disjunctive, one infers from the affirmation of each one to the negation of the other and vice versa, so it produces four conclusions. As for the categorical, it needs in its two premises a common part (juz' mushtarak) between them and a specific part (juz' khass) for each one, so that they correspond; when the common term meets, the required judgment is established between the two premises, and that is the conclusion. It divides into four divisions: [the common term is] predicated in one of them and subject in the other; or predicated in both; or subject in both. The first division divides into what has the common term predicated in the premise whose consequent (tali) is the subject in the conclusion—this is called the first figure; and what is the opposite of that—called the fourth figure, and it is not mentioned in most books due to its being far from natural intuition. As for when the common term is predicated in both, it is called the second figure; and when it is subject in both, it is called the third figure. The principal one is the first, and from it four moods are produced, because the premise that contains the subject in the conclusion must be affirmative universal or particular, and the other must be universal affirmative or negative. The conclusions are four: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative. He mentioned this in very concise words. The second figure he mentioned briefly, and the productive.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage classifies syllogism (qiyas) in the first sense into two main types: conditional (istithna'i) and categorical (iqtirani). The conditional is composed of a conditional premise and a categorical premise that is either the same as one of the parts of the conditional or its contradictory. The conditional is further divided into conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). In the conjunctive, the consequent may be more general than the antecedent; valid inferences include denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent? Actually the text says: one infers from 'the absence of the entailing' and from 'the absence of the necessary'? The author notes the productive and unproductive forms. In the disjunctive, affirming one disjunct negates the other. The categorical syllogism requires a common term between the two premises. It divides into four figures based on whether the common term is predicated or subject in each premise. The first figure occurs when the common term is predicated in the premise whose consequent is the subject in the conclusion. The fourth figure is the reverse and rarely used. The second figure has the common term predicated in both premises, and the third figure has it subject in both. The first figure is the principal one, producing four moods determined by the quantity and quality of the premises, yielding four possible conclusions (A, I, E, O). The second and third figures are mentioned briefly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: In conjunctive conditionals, one can always infer from affirming the consequent.
Correction: The text explicitly says 'la yastadillu min wujud al-lazim' – one does not infer from existence of the necessary (consequent). This is invalid when the consequent is more general.

Checkpoint
How many types of syllogism does the author present in the first division?
A) Two: conditional and categorical
B) Two: conjunctive and disjunctive
C) Three: conjunctive, disjunctive, categorical
D) Four: first, second, third, fourth figure

Key Takeaway
Syllogism is divided into conditional and categorical; conditional syllogisms have specific inference patterns depending on whether they are conjunctive or disjunctive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read the passage on conditional syllogisms. Think of a conditional promise you have made to Allah, such as 'If You grant me this, I will do that.' According to the passage, what are the valid and invalid inferences from such a conditional? Does your consistency in fulfilling the promise align with logical validity? What does that reveal about the integrity of your commitment?
?
The passage distinguishes between productive and unproductive inferences. In your spiritual reasoning, are there arguments you rely on that are logically unsound—like assuming a blessing from Allah guarantees your piety? How can you ensure your reasoning about your faith leads to correct conclusions and actions?
?
The disjunctive syllogism yields four conclusions. Consider a dilemma you face: either you follow your desires or you obey Allah. Using the rule from the passage, if you affirm obedience, you necessarily negate following desires. Conversely, if you negate obedience, you affirm following desires. Which one have you chosen, and what does your life's trajectory indicate about your actual choice? How can you make that choice more explicit and firm?

Figures and Moods of Categorical Syllogism
Figures of categorical syllogism and first figure moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four figures of categorical syllogism and how the first figure produces four moods yielding the four standard categorical conclusions.

Core Idea
Categorical syllogism's figures are determined by the position of the common term; the first figure is principal and yields four moods.

After discussing conditional syllogisms, Imam Razi turns to categorical (iqtirani) syllogisms. He explains that a categorical syllogism requires a common term (juz' mushtarak) shared by both premises. Based on whether this term is predicated or subject in each premise, four figures arise. The first figure is the most natural and productive, yielding four standard moods that cover the four types of categorical propositions (A, I, E, O).

Guided Path
1
Identify the need for a common term
Read how the author states that a categorical syllogism must have a common term (juz' mushtarak) and a specific term (juz' khass) in each premise.
The common term allows the two premises to be connected.
2
Learn the four figures
Trace the four divisions based on whether the common term is predicated or subject in the premises. The first figure: common term predicated in the premise whose consequent is subject in conclusion. Fourth figure: reverse. Second figure: common term predicated in both. Third figure: common term subject in both.
The fourth figure is rarely used and omitted in most books.
3
Focus on the first figure
The first figure is the principal one. Note the condition for its moods: one premise must be affirmative (universal or particular) and the other must be universal (affirmative or negative). This yields four possible conclusions.
The four conclusions are the four categorical propositions: A, I, E, O.
4
Recognize the second and third figures
The author mentions that the second figure is given briefly and the third figure also. Understand that these are less productive or more limited.
The text ends mid-sentence about productive moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القياس بالمعنى الأول ينقسم إلى استثنائي واقتراني، فالاستثنائي ما تركّب من مقدمتين: إحداهما شرطية والأخرى مقرونة بالسكن [؟] وتكون عين أحد طرفي الشرطية أو نقيضها، والاقتراني هو الذي لا يكون كذلك. والاستثنائي ينقسم إلى متصلة ومنفصلة، ففي المتصلة يحتمل أن يكون التالي وهو اللازم أعم من المقدم الذي هو الملزوم كوجود العلم ووجود الحياة، فيستدل من غير الملزوم ومن غير اللازم ولا يستدل من وجود اللازم ولا من عدم الملزوم. وقد أورد ذكر المنتج منها وغير المنتج في كلمات قليلة. وفي المنفصلة يستدل بعين كل واحد على نقيض الآخر وبالعكس فينتج أربع نتائج. وأما الاقتراني فلابد في مقدمتيه من جزء مشترك بينهما ومن جزء خاص لكل واحدة منهما فيتناسبا، وإذا التقى المشترك ثبت الحكم المطلوب بين المقدمتين وهو النتيجة. وينقسم إلى أربعة أقسام: محكوما به في إحداهما محكوما عليه في الأخرى، وإما محكوما به فيهما، وإما محكوما عليه فيهما. والأول ينقسم إلى ما يكون المشترك محكوما به في المقدمة التي يكون التالي من جزئها محكوما عليه في النتيجة ويسمى شكلا أولاً، وإما أن يكون بعكس ذلك ويسمى شكلا رابعا، ولا يورد في أكثر الكتب لبعده عن الطبع. وأما إذا كان المشترك محكوما به فيهما فيسمى شكلا ثانيا، وإذا كان محكوما عليه فيهما يسمى شكلا ثالثا. والعمدة هو الأول وينتج منه أربعة أضرب، لأن المقدمة التي تشتمل على المحكوم عليه في النتيجة يجب أن تكون موجبة كلية أو جزئية، والأخرى يجب أن تكون كلية موجبة أو سالبة، والنتائج أربع: موجبة كلية، موجبة جزئية، سالبة كلية، سالبة جزئية. وقد أورد ذلك في ألفاظ قليلة في غاية الإيجاز. والثاني أورده على سبيل الاختصار، والمنتجة
Then the syllogism in the first sense divides into conditional (istithna'i) and categorical (iqtirani). The conditional is that which is composed of two premises: one of them is conditional and the other is connected with al-sakan [?] and is either the same as one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory. The categorical is that which is not so. The conditional divides into conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). In the conjunctive, it is possible that the consequent (tali), which is the necessary (lazim), is more general than the antecedent (muqaddam), which is the entailing (malzum), such as the existence of knowledge and the existence of life. Thus one infers from the absence of the entailing and from the absence of the necessary, but one does not infer from the existence of the necessary nor from the absence of the entailing. He mentioned the productive and unproductive among them in brief words. In the disjunctive, one infers from the affirmation of each one to the negation of the other and vice versa, so it produces four conclusions. As for the categorical, it needs in its two premises a common part (juz' mushtarak) between them and a specific part (juz' khass) for each one, so that they correspond; when the common term meets, the required judgment is established between the two premises, and that is the conclusion. It divides into four divisions: [the common term is] predicated in one of them and subject in the other; or predicated in both; or subject in both. The first division divides into what has the common term predicated in the premise whose consequent (tali) is the subject in the conclusion—this is called the first figure; and what is the opposite of that—called the fourth figure, and it is not mentioned in most books due to its being far from natural intuition. As for when the common term is predicated in both, it is called the second figure; and when it is subject in both, it is called the third figure. The principal one is the first, and from it four moods are produced, because the premise that contains the subject in the conclusion must be affirmative universal or particular, and the other must be universal affirmative or negative. The conclusions are four: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative. He mentioned this in very concise words. The second figure he mentioned briefly, and the productive.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage classifies syllogism (qiyas) in the first sense into two main types: conditional (istithna'i) and categorical (iqtirani). The conditional is composed of a conditional premise and a categorical premise that is either the same as one of the parts of the conditional or its contradictory. The conditional is further divided into conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). In the conjunctive, the consequent may be more general than the antecedent; valid inferences include denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent? Actually the text says: one infers from 'the absence of the entailing' and from 'the absence of the necessary'? The author notes the productive and unproductive forms. In the disjunctive, affirming one disjunct negates the other. The categorical syllogism requires a common term between the two premises. It divides into four figures based on whether the common term is predicated or subject in each premise. The first figure occurs when the common term is predicated in the premise whose consequent is the subject in the conclusion. The fourth figure is the reverse and rarely used. The second figure has the common term predicated in both premises, and the third figure has it subject in both. The first figure is the principal one, producing four moods determined by the quantity and quality of the premises, yielding four possible conclusions (A, I, E, O). The second and third figures are mentioned briefly.

Second Figure of Categorical Syllogism
Imam Razi states the rules and moods of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the necessary composition of premises and the types of conclusions in the second figure of categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The second figure yields only negative conclusions.

Having covered the first figure, Imam Razi now turns to the second figure. He explains that it must consist of one affirmative and one negative premise, and the premise containing the subject of the conclusion must be universal. The conclusion is always negative. Let us examine the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second figure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the key terms: موجبة, سالبة, كلية, جزئية.
2
Identify the required premise combination
The passage states that the second figure must be composed of an affirmative and a negative premise, and the premise containing the subject of the conclusion must be universal.
This is a strict requirement for the second figure to produce a valid conclusion.
3
Understand the conclusion types
The second figure produces only negative conclusions—either universal negative (سالبة كلية) or particular negative (سالبة جزئية).
No affirmative conclusions are possible in this figure.
4
Reflect on the rationale
The author explains that the two contraries (premises) become like two ends of a contradiction, leading to a negative judgment.
This logical justification helps remember the rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جملة أربعة أضرب أيضاً، ولابد من أن يتألف من موجبة وسالبة والمقدمة التي تشتمل على المحكوم عليه في النتيجة كلية ولا ينتج إلا سالبة مباينة للباقيين دائم الحكم ليكون المتباينان كطرفي النقيض، والنتائج تكون إما سالبة كلية وإما سالبة جزئية. والثالث أيضا أورده على سبيل الاختصار يجب فيه أن يكون المقدمة التي تشتمل على المحكوم عليه في النتيجة موجبة وإحداهما كلية والمنتج منه ستة أضرب كلها جزئية إما موجبة وإما سالبة، وعبر عن الجزئية بقوله يلتقي المحكوم والمحكوم عليه في كل وفي خارج ذلك المحل فربما لا يلتقيان. وأما الشكل الرابع فلم يذكره لما مر، وتفاصيل ذلك يستدعي كلاما طويلا. وغير.
Also a total of four moods, and it must be composed of an affirmative and a negative, and the premise that contains the subject in the conclusion is universal, and it produces only a negative that is contrary to the remaining two, always the judgment, so that the two contraries are like the two ends of contradiction. The conclusions are either universal negative or particular negative. The third [figure] he also mentioned briefly: in it, the premise that contains the subject in the conclusion must be affirmative, and one of them is universal, and it produces six moods, all particular, either affirmative or negative. He expressed the particularity by saying: the subject and predicate meet in every [instance] but outside that locus they might not meet. As for the fourth figure, he did not mention it for the reason mentioned previously, and the details of that require lengthy discussion. And other [things].
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage provides a more detailed discussion of the second and third figures of the categorical syllogism (iqtirani). For the second figure, the author states that it must consist of one affirmative and one negative premise, and the premise containing the subject of the conclusion must be universal. The second figure produces only negative conclusions—either universal or particular. The third figure, also mentioned briefly, requires that the premise containing the subject of the conclusion be affirmative and one of the premises be universal; it yields six moods, all particular in quantity (either affirmative or negative). The author notes that particularity in the third figure is such that the subject and predicate meet in every instance but outside that locus (i.e., the middle term) they might not meet. The fourth figure is omitted because it is far from natural intuition, as stated earlier.

Third Figure and the Fourth Figure Omission
Imam Razi outlines the rules of the third figure and briefly comments on the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rules of the third figure, that it produces only particular conclusions, and why the fourth figure is omitted.

Core Idea
The third figure produces only particular conclusions.

Imam Razi now briefly discusses the third figure, explaining that it yields six moods, all particular in quantity. He also clarifies the nature of particularity in this figure and mentions that the fourth figure is omitted for reasons stated earlier.

Guided Path

2

In the third figure, the premise containing the subject of the conclusion must be affirmative, and one of the premises must be universal.
3

The third figure produces six moods, all particular (جزئية), either affirmative or negative.
Particular means the judgment applies to only some individuals.
4
Interpret the explanation of particularity
The author says that the subject and predicate meet in every instance but outside that locus they might not meet—meaning the middle term is not coextensive, so the conclusion is particular.
This is a technical explanation of why the conclusion cannot be universal.
5

The author mentions that the fourth figure was not discussed because it is unnatural (far from intuition), as previously indicated.
Details of the fourth figure would require lengthy discussion.

The Status of the Non-Existent: Pure Negation
Razi defines the non-existent and defends pure negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological debate over the status of the non-existent and Razi's argument that possible non-existents are pure negation.

Core Idea
For Razi, the possible non-existent is nothing real — pure negation.

The status of non-existent things was a major divide in Islamic theology. Razi enters this debate by first classifying non-existents into impossible and possible, then arguing against those who grant the possible non-existent some reality. This passage presents his position and two proofs from identity and from shared versus specific properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: division, stance, opponent's view, proofs.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two categories and the disagreement
Find the two types of non-existent (impossible and possible) and note who agrees/disagrees with Razi's position that the possible non-existent is pure negation.
Razi's allies include Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri.
3
Analyze the two proofs
The first proof argues that existence is identical to essence for blackness, so blackness cannot be without existence. The second proof uses the distinction between shared affirmation and specific natures to show that affirmation is additional, so when removed, nothing remains.
These proofs target the view that essence and existence are distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية في المعدوم . المعدوم إما أن يكون ممتنع الثبوت ولا نزاع في أنه نفي محض ، وإما أن يكون ممكن الثبوت وهو عندنا وعند أبي الهذيل وأبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة نفي محض خلافا للباقين من المعتزلة ، ومحل الخلاف أنهم زعموا أن وجود السواد زائد على كونه سواداً ثم زعموا أنه يجوز خلو تلك الماهية من صفة الوجود . لنا أن وجود السواد عين كونه سوادا على ما مر فيمتنع أن يكون سوادا مع عدم الوجود ، ولأن السوادية المعدومة مشتركة في الثبوت المقابل للانتفاء المحض ومتباينة بخصوصيتها النوعية ، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز فثبوت تلك الذوات زائد على ماهياتها المخصوصة فهي حال ما فرضناها خالية من صفة الثبوت
The second issue: concerning the non-existent. The non-existent is either impossible of existence, and there is no dispute that it is pure negation, or possible of existence, and it is according to us and according to Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu‘tazila pure negation, contrary to the rest of the Mu‘tazila. The point of disagreement is that they claimed that the existence of blackness is additional to its being black, and then they claimed that it is permissible for that quiddity to be devoid of the attribute of existence. Our proof: the existence of blackness is identical to its being black, as previously mentioned, so it is impossible for it to be black while lacking existence; and because non-existent blacknesses share in the affirmation opposite to pure negation and differ by their specific particularities, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so the affirmation of those essences is additional to their specific quiddities, and they are, when we assume them devoid of the attribute of affirmation, [still something?]
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the status of the non-existent (al-ma‘dūm). Razi divides the non-existent into two kinds: the impossible (mumtani‘) — agreed to be pure negation (nafī muḍ) — and the possible (mumkin). For the latter, Razi (along with Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri of the Mu‘tazila) holds that it is also pure negation, contrary to other Mu‘tazila who say the non-existent possible has some reality (thubūt). The disagreement stems from whether existence is identical to essence or additional. If existence is additional (as opponents hold), then an essence could be devoid of existence and still be something. Razi gives two proofs. First, building on an earlier discussion (page 56?), existence of blackness is identical to its being black; therefore, blackness cannot exist without existence. Second, many non-existent blacknesses share the property of being 'affirmed' (thubūt) as opposed to absolute non-existence, while differing in specific nature. The common factor (affirmation) is distinct from the differentiating factor (specific nature), so the 'affirmation' of these essences is additional to their specific quiddities. Thus, when we assume them devoid of the attribute of affirmation (i.e., existence), they are still something — but Razi is arguing from the opponent's perspective to show inconsistency. The passage concludes that the possible non-existent is pure negation.

Key Terms
المعدوم
ممتنع
ممكن
نفي محض
ثبوت
السواد
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The possible non-existent is a thing with some reality (quiddity) even when it does not exist.
Correction: Razi argues that the possible non-existent is pure negation; it has no reality at all. The opponent's view that existence is additional to essence is refuted by showing that existence is identical to essence, so removing existence removes the thing entirely.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's position on the possible non-existent?
A) It has a kind of reality intermediate between existence and non-existence
B) It is pure negation, like the impossible
C) It is an essence that can exist or not exist
D) It is a mental concept only

Key Takeaway
Razi holds that possible non-existents are pure negation, not things with any reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very essence is nothing without the existence that God grants you every moment, what claim do you have to any independent worth or power? How does this strip away your pride?
?
Razi argues that the possible non-existent is pure negation—utterly dependent on the Creator for any reality. When you consider your own contingent existence, does it fill your heart with gratitude and humble reliance on Allah, or do you live as if your existence is self-sufficient?
?
The opposing view grants the non-existent some 'thingness' apart from God. If you were to think of your own potential non-existence as having some reality apart from God, how might that diminish your sense of absolute need for Him? Can you truly say 'there is no god but Allah' if you imagine your own quiddity has a shadow of independence?

Razi's Critique of Three Answers on Common Existence
Razi (as editor) criticizes three answers given to earlier objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi demonstrates the failure of three proposed answers to objections about the commonality of existence.

Core Idea
Razi exposes why the three answers fail to resolve the commonality problem.

Imam Razi, in his editorial role, scrutinizes three answers offered to counter objections about a common existence. He argues that each answer misses the point—the first confuses specific and absolute realization, the second inadvertently rejects the very division it tries to support, and the third leads to an infinite regress. This lesson unpacks his critique step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice that Razi addresses three separate answers in sequence.
The critique is dense; focus on the logical structure rather than every word.
2
Analyze the first critique
Razi argues that the first answer fails because it does not address the commonality of absolute realization. Understand why specific removals/realizations do not solve the problem of a shared existence.
3
Analyze the second critique
The second answer is a rejection of the division of existence into necessary and possible, not a proper response. Razi shows it presupposes what it tries to prove.
4
Analyze the third critique
The third answer proposes a second existence shared between existence and a substance stripped of existence. Razi finds this inconceivable and shows it leads to an infinite regress.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how these critiques deepen the problem of common existence. What does Razi’s editorial role tell us about the dialectical method in kalam?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : قوله في الجواب الأول أن ارتفاع كل ماهية يقابل تحققها ليس جوابا عن الأول ، فإن ذلك لا ينا فى الأول بيانه أن ارتفاع (۱) يقابل تحقق (۱) وارتفاع ( ب ) يقابل تحقق (ب) فالارتفاع المطلق المحمول عليهما وعلى غيرهما أمر مشترك ، ويقابله حقق مشترك يصح أن يحمل على كل تحقق خاص بأحدهما وبغيرهما ، ونحن لا نمنى باشتراك الوجود إلا ذلك التحقق المطلق لاهذا التحقق ولا ذاك التحقق . وقوله في الجواب الثانى : ليس جوابا من قوله الوجود ينقسم إلى واجب و ممكن. فإن الذي فسره به هو رد تلك القسمة في قوله ، وهو أن بقاء تلك الماهية الخاصة إما أن يكون واجبا أولا يكون هو الموجود ، فإن البقاء هو استمرار الوجود، وكأنه يقول استمرار وجود تلك الماهية كذا وكذا ولولا أن استمرار الوجود مشترك بين الواجب وغيره الا صحت هذه القسمة . وقوله فى الجواب الثالث: أيضاً ليس بجواب عن الحجة الثالثة ، فإنه لا يتصور اشتراك وجود ثان بين الوجود وبين الجوهر العارى عن الوجود واللاوجود حتى إذا تغير في التصور وتبدل أحدهما بالآخر نفى ذلك الوجود المشترك ولزم منه أن يكون للوجود وجود آخر .
I say: His statement in the first answer that the removal of every quiddity corresponds to its realization is not an answer to the first [objection]; because that does not prevent [؟] in the first, its explanation is that the removal of (a) corresponds to the realization of (a), and the removal of (b) corresponds to the realization of (b), so the absolute removal predicated on both and on others is a common matter, and it is met by a common realization that can be predicated on every specific realization of one and of others, and we do not mean by the commonality of existence except that absolute realization, not this realization nor that realization. And his statement in the second answer: it is not an answer from his saying that existence is divided into necessary and possible. For what he interpreted it with is a rejection of that division in his saying, which is that the continuance of that specific quiddity is either necessary or it is not the existent, for continuance is the persistence of existence, and it is as if he says the persistence of the existence of that quiddity is such and such, and were it not that the persistence of existence is common between the necessary and other than it, this division would not be valid. And his statement in the third answer: also it is not an answer to the third proof; for it is not conceivable that a second existence is shared between existence and the substance stripped of existence and non-existence, such that when it changes in conception and one is replaced by the other, that common existence is negated, and it would follow that existence has another existence.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is an editorial critique by Imam Razi himself (as the commentator on his own work) of three answers given to earlier objections. He argues that the first answer fails because it does not address the commonality of existence: the removal and realization of each thing are specific, but the absolute removal is common, and that common realization is what is meant by existence. The second answer is a rejection of the division, not an answer: the opponent already accepts that persistence of existence is common. The third answer is also inadequate: positing a second common existence between existence and a substance stripped of both existence and non-existence is inconceivable; the substitution scenario would negate that common existence and lead to an infinite regress.

Key Terms
الارتفاع
التحقق
الوجود المشترك
البقاء
استمرار الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three answers successfully refute objections about a common existence.
Correction: Razi critically shows each answer fails: the first confuses specific and absolute, the second rejects rather than answers, and the third leads to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the editorial note, what is the main problem with the first answer?
A) It assumes that removal is common
B) It fails to address the commonality of existence
C) It leads to infinite regress
D) It confuses quiddity with existence

Key Takeaway
Razi's editorial critique reveals that each proposed answer fails to solve the problem of common existence, underscoring the difficulty of the issue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone challenges a belief you hold dear, do you immediately offer a ready-made answer without checking whether it truly meets the objection? Razi shows how three common answers fail to solve a deep problem. In your own life, when defending your faith, do you take the time to examine whether your response actually addresses the core issue, or do you settle for a comforting but logically inadequate reply? Commit today to seek out and resolve the real difficulties in your convictions, rather than hiding behind shallow answers.
?
Are you willing to let go of an argument you once found persuasive when you see it is logically flawed? Razi's critiques are sharp and uncomfortable. When you read his words, do you feel a resistance to being corrected? Do you humbly accept logical refutation, or do you cling to your own reasoning even when it is shown inadequate? This lesson calls you to a standard of truth-seeking that demands intellectual humility. Examine yourself: is your attachment to being right stronger than your love for truth?
?
The third answer leads to an infinite regress—a clear absurdity. Have you ever held a belief that, if pushed, would lead to an infinite regress or contradiction? Perhaps in your understanding of God's attributes or the nature of existence. This lesson challenges you to press your own beliefs to their logical conclusions. Are you afraid of what you might find? Or are you ready to submit to the truth, even if it means revising deeply held ideas? Pray for the courage to face logical scrutiny and to abandon any error, no matter how familiar.

The Non-Existent: Definition and Dispute
Razi defines the non-existent and presents the theological disagreement about its status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of the non-existent and the theological disagreement about whether the possible non-existent is pure negation or has some reality.

Core Idea
The non-existent is either impossible (pure negation) or possible, but scholars disagree whether the possible non-existent has any reality.

Razi begins the second question of his discussion by clarifying what 'non-existent' means. He distinguishes between the impossible non-existent, which everyone agrees is pure nothingness, and the possible non-existent, which becomes a point of contention. This passage sets the stage for a key debate in kalam: does the non-existent have any kind of reality? Let's examine the definition and the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without attempting to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the two-part structure: a definition and a dispute.
The passage is concise, so read carefully.
2
Identify the two types of non-existent
Razi divides the non-existent into two: the impossible (mumtani') and the possible (mumkin). Underline these terms. Note that the first type is uncontroversial.
3
Understand the disagreement
For the possible non-existent, Razi states his view (with two Mu'tazilites) that it is pure negation, contra the majority Mu'tazilites. Identify the parties and the claim.
The majority Mu'tazilites think the possible non-existent has some reality.
4
Grasp the basis of the dispute
The disagreement stems from whether existence is an additional attribute to the essence. The Mu'tazilites (majority) say yes, so they think the essence can exist without existence, giving it reality even when non-existent.
This is a subtle metaphysical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية في المعدوم . المعدوم إما أن يكون ممتنع الثبوت ولا نزاع في أنه نفى محض ، وإما أن يكون ممكن الثبوت وهو عندنا وعند أبي الهذيل وأبي الحسين البصرى من المعتزلة نفى محض خلافا للباقين من المعتزلة ، ومحل الخلاف أنهم زعموا أن وجود المواد زائد على كونه سواداً ثم زعموا أنه يجوز خلو تلك الماهية من صفة الوجود
The second question: on the non-existent. The non-existent is either impossible of realization, and there is no dispute that it is pure negation; or it is possible of realization, and it is according to us and according to Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri of the Mu'tazilites pure negation, contrary to the rest of the Mu'tazilites. The locus of disagreement is that they claimed that the existence of substances is additional to their being black, then they claimed that it is permissible for that quiddity to be devoid of the attribute of existence.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the subject of the second question: the non-existent (al-ma'dum). It divides the non-existent into two types: the impossible (mumtani' al-thubut), which is pure negation with no dispute; and the possible (mumkin al-thubut), about which there is disagreement. Razi states his own position (with Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri) that the possible non-existent is also pure negation, opposing the majority of Mu'tazilites who hold that the non-existent has some reality. The basis of their disagreement is that the Mu'tazilites believe existence is an additional attribute to the essence (e.g., blackness), so they think it is possible for the essence to exist without the attribute of existence, meaning the essence has a reality in itself even when non-existent.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الممتنع
الممكن
الثبوت
النفي المحض
صفة الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazilites agree that the non-existent is something real.
Correction: Razi points out that Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (both Mu'tazilites) agree with him that the possible non-existent is pure negation.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the status of the possible non-existent for the majority of Mu'tazilites?
A) It is pure negation
B) It is a thing with some reality
C) It is impossible
D) It is an attribute of existence

Key Takeaway
The non-existent is either impossible (pure negation) or possible; the dispute over the possible non-existent hinges on whether existence is an additional attribute to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own existence were merely a gift from Allah and you have no inherent reality, would you still pride yourself on your achievements? How does knowing that you were 'pure negation' before He brought you into being change the way you see your own worth?
?
When you plan for the future, do you act as if those possibilities are already 'real' or do you acknowledge that they are nothing without Allah's will? What commitment can you make today to submit your hopes to Him, knowing that the possible non-existent is pure negation?
?
The passage shows that even the most learned scholars disagreed about the nature of the non-existent. How much do you truly trust Allah's knowledge and power over what does not yet exist? In what area of your life are you trying to 'add existence' to your plans by your own effort, rather than relying on Him?

The Commonality of the Non-Existent and Its Distinctions
Razi explains the implication of commonality and the distinctions between existent, established, non-existent, negated, and states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the non-existent is common to impossible and possible, and how those who affirm the non-existent as a thing distinguish between existent, established, non-existent, and negated, including the intermediary of states.

Core Idea
The non-existent is a broader category that includes both possible and impossible, and requires careful distinctions.

In this editorial note, Imam Razi clarifies an implication of the previous discussion: if the non-existent is common between impossible and possible, then its opposite, existence, is common between necessary and possible. He then explains the nuanced positions of those who affirm that the non-existent is a thing, introducing key distinctions that avoid conflating categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on commonality
Carefully read the first part where Razi states the implication of the non-existent being common. Notice how he links this to the opposite category.
2
Identify the four terms and their relations
Pay attention to the terms: existent (موجود), established (ثابت), non-existent (معدوم), and negated (منفي). Understand how they relate: every existent is established, but not vice versa; the impossible is called negated, not non-existent.
The term 'established' (thābit) is sometimes confused with 'existent' but is broader.
3
Understand the intermediary (ḥāl)
Learn about the 'state' (ḥāl) that is neither existent nor non-existent, but an intermediary. This is a key concept for those who affirm the non-existent as a thing.
This concept allows for a middle ground between existence and non-existence.
4
Reflect on the overall position
Consider how this position avoids the problem of calling the impossible 'non-existent' and instead reserves that term for possible non-existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : اعترف ههنا بأن المعدوم مشترك بين الممتنع والممكن ، ويلزمه من ذلك اشتراك مقابله بين الواجب والممكن ، وينبغى أن يعلم أن القائلين بأن المعدوم شيء يفرقون بين الموجود والنابت وبين المعدوم والمنفى ، ويقولون كل موجود ثابت ولا ينعكس وبثبوت واسطة بين الموجو والم دوم ، ولا يجوزون بين النابت والمنفى واسطة ولا يقولون للممتنع معدوم ، بل يقولون إنه منفى، ويقولون للذوات التي لا تكون موجودة شىء وثابت ، وللتعينات التى لا تقبل إلا مع الذوات حال لا موجود ولا معدوم بل هي وسائط بينهما، والبصريون من هذا الجبار يقولون الذرات في من مشايخهم كأبي على وأبى هاشم والقاضى عبد الجبار وأتباعهم يقولون بأن الذوات في العدم جواهر وأعراض وأبو القاسم البلخي والبغداديون يقولون بأنها أشياء والفاعل يجعلها جواهر وأعراضا .
I say: He acknowledged here that the non-existent is common between the impossible and the possible, and it follows from that the commonality of its opposite between the necessary and the possible. And it should be known that those who say the non-existent is a thing distinguish between the existent and the established [؟] and between the non-existent and the negated. They say: every existent is established, but not conversely, and there is an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent. And they do not allow an intermediary between the established and the negated, and they do not call the impossible non-existent; rather they say it is negated. And they say for essences that are not existent: they are a thing and established. And for the determinations that are only accepted together with essences, they are a state (ḥāl) neither existent nor non-existent, but rather intermediaries between them. And the Basrans of this strong [؟] say: the atoms are from [؟] their teachers like Abu Ali and Abu Hashim and Qadi Abd al-Jabbar and their followers say that the essences in non-existence are substances and accidents. And Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi and the Baghdadis say that they are things, and the agent makes them substances and accidents.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This editorial note explains an implication of the previous passage: if the non-existent is common between impossible and possible, then its opposite (existent) is common between necessary and possible. Razi then clarifies the views of those who affirm that the non-existent is a thing. They distinguish between 'existent' and 'established' (thābit), and between 'non-existent' (ma'dūm) and 'negated' (manfī). They hold that every existent is established, but not vice versa: there is an intermediary between existent and non-existent. They do not call the impossible 'non-existent' but 'negated'. Possible non-existent essences are 'things' and 'established'. Furthermore, they posit 'states' (aḥwāl) as intermediaries between existence and non-existence, which are neither existent nor non-existent. Then Razi describes the Basran Mu'tazilites (like Abu Ali, Abu Hashim, Qadi Abd al-Jabbar) who hold that essences in non-existence are substances and accidents, while al-Balkhi and the Baghdadis say they are mere 'things' and the agent (God) makes them substances and accidents.

Key Terms
المعدوم
المشترك
الموجود
الثابت
المنفي
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all non-existent things are simply 'nothing' or that the impossible is merely non-existent.
Correction: This passage shows that those who affirm the non-existent as a thing distinguish between the impossible (called negated) and possible non-existents (called non-existent and established). There is also an intermediary state (ḥāl) that is neither existent nor non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the proponents say about the impossible?
A) It is non-existent
B) It is negated
C) It is a thing
D) It is an intermediary

Key Takeaway
The non-existent is common to impossible and possible, but careful distinctions (existent/established, non-existent/negated, and states) are needed to avoid conflating categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered something as impossible in your life when in reality it was merely non-existent and possible? How does the distinction between 'non-existent' and 'negated' challenge you to reconsider your assumptions about what Allah can do?
?
The passage teaches that 'every existent is established, but not every established is existent.' Is there an area of your faith where you only value what is physically present, neglecting the reality of unseen truths that are established in Allah's knowledge?
?
When you face a loss or absence, do you tend to dismiss it as nothing, or do you affirm its reality as a non-existent possible? How can this theological precision transform your patience and hope?

Basran vs. Baghdadi Views on Essences in Non-Existence
Razi outlines the differing views of Basran and Baghdadi scholars on the status of essences in non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Basran Mu'tazilites and the Baghdadis regarding whether essences in non-existence are substances and accidents or mere things.

Core Idea
Even among those who affirm the non-existent as a thing, there is disagreement: Basrans say essences are substances and accidents, Baghdadis say they are mere things.

After explaining the general position of those who affirm the non-existent as a thing, Razi highlights a further division: the Basran school (led by Abu Ali, Abu Hashim, Qadi Abd al-Jabbar) holds that essences in non-existence are already substances and accidents, while the Baghdadis (al-Balkhi and others) say they are merely 'things' that God then makes into substances and accidents. This sectarian note illustrates the range of opinion within the Mu'tazilite tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sectarian views
Read the final part of the note where Razi mentions the Basrans and Baghdadis. Identify the two groups and their respective positions.
The Basrans include Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, and Qadi Abd al-Jabbar.
2
Compare the two positions
Contrast the Basran claim that essences in non-existence are substances and accidents with the Baghdadi claim that they are mere things. Ask: what difference does it make?
This reflects a deeper ontological debate about the nature of non-existent entities.
3
Consider the role of the agent
Notice that for the Baghdadis, the agent (God) makes the things into substances and accidents, implying that in non-existence they lack those properties.
4
Reflect on the diversity of views
Recognize that even within a single school (Mu'tazilism), there are significant disagreements on fundamental metaphysical issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi and the Baghdadis say that they are things, and the agent makes them substances and accidents.

Razi then clarifies the views of those who affirm that the non-existent is a thing. Proof 1: Existence Identical to Essence
Razi's first proof that the non-existent is pure negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first argument that a non-existent blackness cannot be black because existence is identical to its essence, and that the shared 'realization' among non-existent blacknesses is additional, making them pure negation when removed.

Core Idea
Blackness without existence is impossible because blackness just is existence.

Razi begins his case against the reality of the non-existent with a direct proof: if existence is identical to essence—as established earlier—then a non-existent blackness would be a contradiction in terms. The argument unpacks what it means for multiple non-existent blacknesses to share a ‘realization’ that is additional to their specific quiddities, showing that when stripped of that realization, they are nothing.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof in Arabic and translation
Read the passage once to get the flow of the argument. Note the key terms: السواد, الثبوت, الانتفاء المحض.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The proof relies on the earlier thesis that existence is identical to the quiddity of blackness. From that, it follows that blackness cannot exist without existence. Then it argues that shared ‘realization’ among non-existent blacknesses is additional, so without it they are pure negation.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If existence is identical to essence, does that mean every existing thing is essentially what it is? How does that show non-existents are not things?
The key is that 'realization' (thubut) is not just existence but something additional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن وجود السواد عين كونه سوادا على ما مر فيمتنع أن يكون سوادا من عدم الوجود ، ولأن السوادية المعدومة مشتركة فى الثبوت المقابل للانتفاء المحض ومتباينة بخصوصيتها النوعية ، ومابه الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز فثبوت تلك الذوات زائد على ماهياتها المخصوصة فهي حال ما فرضناها خالية من صفة الثبوت (۱) ولان عدد الذوات المعدودة قابل للزيادة والنقصان فيكون متناهيا والخصم لا يقول به (۲) ولأن الذوات أزلية فلا تكون مقدورة والوجوه حال عندهم فلا يكون مقدوراً عندهم، وإذا لم يقع الذات ولا الوجود بالفاعل كانت الذات الموجودة غنية عن الفاعل (۳) ولان السواد المعدوم إما أن يكون واحداً أو كثيراً فإن كان واحداً فالوحدة إن كانت لازمة للماهية امتنع زوالها فوجب أن لا يتعدد في الوجود ، وإن لم تكن لازمة فيفرض ارتفاعها ، لأن كل ما كان ممكنا لا يلزم من فرض ارتفاعه محال ، فإذا زالت الوحدة حصل التعدد وهو لا يتحقق إلا إذا تباين الشيئان بالهوية ، ثم ما به التباين إن كان من لوازم الماهية فكل شيئين فهما مختلفان بالماهية هذا خلف، وإن لم يكن من لوازمها كان الشيء حال عدمه موردا للصفات المتزايلة ، ولو جاز ذلك الجاز أن يكون محل الحركات والسكنات المتعاقبة عدما محضا وذلك عين السفسطة (٤)
For us [proof]: that the existence of blackness is identical with its being black, as has passed, so it is impossible for it to be black in the absence of existence. And because the blacknesses that are non-existent share in the realization (thubūt) opposed to pure negation and are differentiated by their specific particularities, and that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished, so the realization of those essences is additional to their specific quiddities, and they are, when we assume them devoid of the attribute of realization (1). And because the number of counted essences is susceptible to increase and decrease, so it is finite, and the opponent does not say so (2). And because the essences are eternal, so they cannot be caused, and the modes (wujūh) are states (ḥāl) according to them, so they cannot be caused according to them. And if neither the essence nor existence is effected by the agent, then the existing essence would be independent of the agent (3). And because the non-existent blackness is either one or many. If it is one, then if unity is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, its removal is impossible, so it must not become multiple in existence. And if it is not a necessary concomitant, then we suppose its removal, because for everything possible, supposing its removal is not impossible. So when unity is removed, multiplicity occurs, and it is not realized except when two things are distinct by identity. Then that by which they are distinct: if it is among the necessary concomitants of the quiddity, then any two things are different in quiddity — this is contrary [to the assumption]. And if it is not among its necessary concomitants, then the thing, in the state of its non-existence, would be a locus for successive attributes. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible that a pure non-existence be a locus for successive motions and rests, and that is pure sophistry (4).
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents four proofs that the possible non-existent is pure negation. First proof: existence is identical to essence (e.g., blackness), so a non-existent blackness cannot be black; the shared 'realization' among non-existent blacknesses is additional to their specific quiddities, so when assumed without that realization, they are nothing. Second proof: the number of non-existent essences can increase or decrease, implying finitude; but the opponent claims they are infinite? Actually, the opponent may not accept finitude. Third proof: if essences are eternal in non-existence, they cannot be caused by an agent; but the opponent may claim that the agent acts upon these essences to bring them into existence. If the agent does not cause either the essence or its existence, the existing essence would be independent of the agent. Fourth proof: a non-existent blackness is either one or many. If it is one and unity is necessary, it cannot become many in existence; if unity is not necessary, we can suppose its removal, leading to multiplicity. For two things to be distinct, there must be a differentiator. If that differentiator is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, then any two things would differ in quiddity, which is false. If it is not a necessary concomitant, then the thing in its state of non-existence would bear successive attributes, which is purely sophistical.

Key Terms
وجود السواد
عين كونه سوادا
الثبوت
الانتفاء المحض
الذوات المعدودة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes the identity thesis.
Correction: Razi is not proving the identity thesis here; he is building on it as already established. The proof shows the consequences of that thesis for non-existents.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first proof, what happens when we assume non-existent blacknesses devoid of the attribute of realization (thubut)?
A) They become black
B) They become something else
C) They become pure negation
D) They become existence

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof uses the identity of existence and essence to argue that non-existents lack any reality; stripped of added attributes, they are pure negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is identical to your essence, then what are you without your specific qualities—your personality, achievements, relationships? When you strip away everything that distinguishes you, are you still 'you'? How does this force you to re-evaluate your self-worth before your Creator?
?
This proof reduces non-existents to pure negation. Consider your own sins or failings that you wish you could undo—are they 'things' with a lingering reality? Or does this teaching free you to see them as pure negation, vanished in the face of God's forgiveness? What would it mean for you to truly believe that what is gone is nothing?
?
Razi argues that thubut (realization) is an added attribute. If your very 'realness' is a gift from God, not something intrinsic, how does that reshape your sense of dependence on Him? Pause and ask: If God withdrew His sustaining thubut from you this moment, would anything of 'you' remain? What does that demand of your heart right now?

Proof 2: Finitude of Non-Existent Essences
Razi's second proof: finitude of counted essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second argument that the number of non-existent essences must be finite, contrary to the opponent's view, thus showing they are not real things.

Core Idea
Non-existent essences, if countable, cannot be infinite—proving they are not things.

Razi's second proof is concise: if non-existent essences are regarded as countable entities, their number can increase or decrease, implying finitude. Since the opponent denies the finitude of such essences, the premise that they are countable things leads to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the short proof
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key terms: عدد الذوات المعدودة, قابل للزيادة والنقصان, متناهيا.
2
Analyze the argument structure
The proof assumes that non-existent essences can be counted (الذوات المعدودة). Their number can increase or decrease, which entails finitude. The opponent does not hold that they are finite, so the assumption leads to a contradiction.
3
Consider the opponent's position
Who is the opponent? Possibly those who claim non-existents are eternal and infinite. Razi argues that if they are countable, they must be finite, contradicting their supposed eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن وجود السواد عين كونه سوادا على ما مر فيمتنع أن يكون سوادا من عدم الوجود ، ولأن السوادية المعدومة مشتركة فى الثبوت المقابل للانتفاء المحض ومتباينة بخصوصيتها النوعية ، ومابه الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز فثبوت تلك الذوات زائد على ماهياتها المخصوصة فهي حال ما فرضناها خالية من صفة الثبوت (۱) ولان عدد الذوات المعدودة قابل للزيادة والنقصان فيكون متناهيا والخصم لا يقول به (۲) ولأن الذوات أزلية فلا تكون مقدورة والوجوه حال عندهم فلا يكون مقدوراً عندهم، وإذا لم يقع الذات ولا الوجود بالفاعل كانت الذات الموجودة غنية عن الفاعل (۳) ولان السواد المعدوم إما أن يكون واحداً أو كثيراً فإن كان واحداً فالوحدة إن كانت لازمة للماهية امتنع زوالها فوجب أن لا يتعدد في الوجود ، وإن لم تكن لازمة فيفرض ارتفاعها ، لأن كل ما كان ممكنا لا يلزم من فرض ارتفاعه محال ، فإذا زالت الوحدة حصل التعدد وهو لا يتحقق إلا إذا تباين الشيئان بالهوية ، ثم ما به التباين إن كان من لوازم الماهية فكل شيئين فهما مختلفان بالماهية هذا خلف، وإن لم يكن من لوازمها كان الشيء حال عدمه موردا للصفات المتزايلة ، ولو جاز ذلك الجاز أن يكون محل الحركات والسكنات المتعاقبة عدما محضا وذلك عين السفسطة (٤)
For us [proof]: that the existence of blackness is identical with its being black, as has passed, so it is impossible for it to be black in the absence of existence. And because the blacknesses that are non-existent share in the realization (thubūt) opposed to pure negation and are differentiated by their specific particularities, and that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished, so the realization of those essences is additional to their specific quiddities, and they are, when we assume them devoid of the attribute of realization (1). And because the number of counted essences is susceptible to increase and decrease, so it is finite, and the opponent does not say so (2). And because the essences are eternal, so they cannot be caused, and the modes (wujūh) are states (ḥāl) according to them, so they cannot be caused according to them. And if neither the essence nor existence is effected by the agent, then the existing essence would be independent of the agent (3). And because the non-existent blackness is either one or many. If it is one, then if unity is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, its removal is impossible, so it must not become multiple in existence. And if it is not a necessary concomitant, then we suppose its removal, because for everything possible, supposing its removal is not impossible. So when unity is removed, multiplicity occurs, and it is not realized except when two things are distinct by identity. Then that by which they are distinct: if it is among the necessary concomitants of the quiddity, then any two things are different in quiddity — this is contrary [to the assumption]. And if it is not among its necessary concomitants, then the thing, in the state of its non-existence, would be a locus for successive attributes. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible that a pure non-existence be a locus for successive motions and rests, and that is pure sophistry (4).

Commentary
This passage presents four proofs that the possible non-existent is pure negation. First proof: existence is identical to essence (e.g., blackness), so a non-existent blackness cannot be black; the shared 'realization' among non-existent blacknesses is additional to their specific quiddities, so when assumed without that realization, they are nothing. Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude from the fact that the number of non-existent essences can increase or decrease?
A) They are infinite
B) They are finite
C) They are not countable
D) They are eternal

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof uses the susceptibility to increase and decrease to show that non-existent essences, if countable, must be finite—contradicting the opponent's view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that a belief can be internally inconsistent. Is there a belief you hold that leads to a conclusion you refuse to accept? Will you honestly follow that belief to its logical end—even if it makes you uncomfortable?
?
The opponent denies finitude because they want non-existent essences to be infinite. Is there a desire or fear in your life that makes you deny an obvious truth? What is it, and how will you confront it today?
?
This argument reduces abstract things to countable numbers. Do you treat your own thoughts and ideas as 'things'—making them fixed and countable? Could you be clinging to a mental construct that, if examined, would have to be finite or even nonexistent? Will you examine it now?

Proof 3: Eternity and Independence from Agent
Razi's third proof: eternity leads to independence from agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third argument that if non-existent essences are eternal, they cannot be caused by an agent, which would make existing essences independent of God—an absurdity.

Core Idea
Eternal non-existent essences cannot be caused, making existing things independent of the agent.

Razi's third proof targets the opponent who claims that non-existent essences are eternal. If they are eternal, they are not caused by any agent. But existing essences are composites of essence and existence; if neither is caused by the agent, the existing essence would be independent of God—contradicting the need for a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic and translation. Note: الذوات أزلية, مقدورة, الوجوه حال.
2
Break down the premise
Razi states that non-existent essences are eternal (أزلية). Therefore, they cannot be caused (مقدورة). He adds that the modes (wujūh) are states (ḥāl) according to the opponent, so they also cannot be caused.
3
Draw the conclusion
If neither the essence nor existence is effected by the agent, then the existing essence (the composite) would be independent of the agent, which is false. So the original premise (eternity of essences) must be rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن وجود السواد عين كونه سوادا على ما مر فيمتنع أن يكون سوادا من عدم الوجود ، ولأن السوادية المعدومة مشتركة فى الثبوت المقابل للانتفاء المحض ومتباينة بخصوصيتها النوعية ، ومابه الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز فثبوت تلك الذوات زائد على ماهياتها المخصوصة فهي حال ما فرضناها خالية من صفة الثبوت (۱) ولان عدد الذوات المعدودة قابل للزيادة والنقصان فيكون متناهيا والخصم لا يقول به (۲) ولأن الذوات أزلية فلا تكون مقدورة والوجوه حال عندهم فلا يكون مقدوراً عندهم، وإذا لم يقع الذات ولا الوجود بالفاعل كانت الذات الموجودة غنية عن الفاعل (۳) ولان السواد المعدوم إما أن يكون واحداً أو كثيراً فإن كان واحداً فالوحدة إن كانت لازمة للماهية امتنع زوالها فوجب أن لا يتعدد في الوجود ، وإن لم تكن لازمة فيفرض ارتفاعها ، لأن كل ما كان ممكنا لا يلزم من فرض ارتفاعه محال ، فإذا زالت الوحدة حصل التعدد وهو لا يتحقق إلا إذا تباين الشيئان بالهوية ، ثم ما به التباين إن كان من لوازم الماهية فكل شيئين فهما مختلفان بالماهية هذا خلف، وإن لم يكن من لوازمها كان الشيء حال عدمه موردا للصفات المتزايلة ، ولو جاز ذلك الجاز أن يكون محل الحركات والسكنات المتعاقبة عدما محضا وذلك عين السفسطة (٤)
For us [proof]: that the existence of blackness is identical with its being black, as has passed, so it is impossible for it to be black in the absence of existence. And because the blacknesses that are non-existent share in the realization (thubūt) opposed to pure negation and are differentiated by their specific particularities, and that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished, so the realization of those essences is additional to their specific quiddities, and they are, when we assume them devoid of the attribute of realization (1). And because the number of counted essences is susceptible to increase and decrease, so it is finite, and the opponent does not say so (2). And because the essences are eternal, so they cannot be caused, and the modes (wujūh) are states (ḥāl) according to them, so they cannot be caused according to them. And if neither the essence nor existence is effected by the agent, then the existing essence would be independent of the agent (3). And because the non-existent blackness is either one or many. If it is one, then if unity is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, its removal is impossible, so it must not become multiple in existence. And if it is not a necessary concomitant, then we suppose its removal, because for everything possible, supposing its removal is not impossible. So when unity is removed, multiplicity occurs, and it is not realized except when two things are distinct by identity. Then that by which they are distinct: if it is among the necessary concomitants of the quiddity, then any two things are different in quiddity — this is contrary [to the assumption]. And if it is not among its necessary concomitants, then the thing, in the state of its non-existence, would be a locus for successive attributes. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible that a pure non-existence be a locus for successive motions and rests, and that is pure sophistry (4).

Commentary
This passage presents four proofs that the possible non-existent is pure negation. First proof: existence is identical to essence (e.g., blackness), so a non-existent blackness cannot be black; the shared 'realization' among non-existent blacknesses is additional to their specific quiddities, so when assumed without that realization, they are nothing. Third proof: if essences are eternal in non-existence, they cannot be caused by an agent; but the opponent may claim that the agent acts upon these essences to bring them into existence. If it is one and unity is necessary, it cannot become many in existence; if unity is not necessary, we can suppose its removal, leading to multiplicity. For two things to be distinct, there must be a differentiator. If that differentiator is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, then any two things would differ in quiddity, which is false. If it is not a necessary concomitant, then the thing in its state of non-existence would bear successive attributes, which is purely sophistical.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that the agent causes both essence and existence separately.
Correction: Razi is arguing from the opponent's premises: if essences are eternal and existence is a mode inhering in them, then the agent has no role.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's third proof, what follows if non-existent essences are eternal?
A) They are caused by God
B) They are not caused
C) They are finite
D) They are identical to existence

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof shows that the opponent's claim of eternal non-existent essences leads to the absurd conclusion that existing things are independent of the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as if your existence is self-evident and not dependent on God every moment? What would change in your attitude if you repeatedly affirmed that none of your attributes—not even your consciousness—are inherent in you, but are given by God?
?
This proof shows that if anything were eternal by itself, it would be independent of God. What in your life do you treat as 'eternal' or 'fixed' without reference to God—your personality, your relationships, your health? Will you consciously trace each of these back to God's creative act today?
?
If you truly believed that your very existence is a mode that could not exist without an agent, how would it affect your sense of entitlement? Are there areas where you feel you 'deserve' something, forgetting that you and your merits are entirely sustained by God?

Proof 4: Unity and Multiplicity of Non-Existent
Razi's fourth proof: dilemma of unity and multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourth and most detailed argument that a non-existent blackness cannot be one or many without leading to contradictions or absurdity, proving it must be pure negation.

Core Idea
A non-existent blackness cannot be one or many without absurd consequences—proving it is nothing.

Razi's fourth proof is a complex reductio ad absurdum. He considers a non-existent blackness and examines whether it is one or many. If it is one and unity is necessary, it cannot become many in existence. If unity is not necessary, we can remove it, leading to multiplicity. But then the differentiator must be a concomitant of the quiddity or not—both options lead to contradictions or sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete proof
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This proof has several sub-arguments.
2
Follow the first branch: unity is necessary
If non-existent blackness is one and unity is a necessary concomitant, then it cannot become many in existence. But we see blackness can be multiple (e.g., multiple black objects). So unity is not necessary.
3
Follow the second branch: unity is not necessary
If unity is not necessary, we can suppose its removal. Then multiplicity arises, which requires a differentiator between two distinct things. That differentiator is either a necessary concomitant of the quiddity or not.
4
Examine the differentiator options
If the differentiator is a necessary concomitant, then any two things differ in quiddity, which is false (e.g., two black things are both blackness). If it is not necessary, then the non-existent blackness would bear successive attributes, making pure non-existence a locus for accidents—pure sophistry.
5
Conclude the argument
All options lead to contradiction or absurdity, so the initial assumption that non-existent blackness is a thing must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن وجود السواد عين كونه سوادا على ما مر فيمتنع أن يكون سوادا من عدم الوجود ، ولأن السوادية المعدومة مشتركة فى الثبوت المقابل للانتفاء المحض ومتباينة بخصوصيتها النوعية ، ومابه الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز فثبوت تلك الذوات زائد على ماهياتها المخصوصة فهي حال ما فرضناها خالية من صفة الثبوت (۱) ولان عدد الذوات المعدودة قابل للزيادة والنقصان فيكون متناهيا والخصم لا يقول به (۲) ولأن الذوات أزلية فلا تكون مقدورة والوجوه حال عندهم فلا يكون مقدوراً عندهم، وإذا لم يقع الذات ولا الوجود بالفاعل كانت الذات الموجودة غنية عن الفاعل (۳) ولان السواد المعدوم إما أن يكون واحداً أو كثيراً فإن كان واحداً فالوحدة إن كانت لازمة للماهية امتنع زوالها فوجب أن لا يتعدد في الوجود ، وإن لم تكن لازمة فيفرض ارتفاعها ، لأن كل ما كان ممكنا لا يلزم من فرض ارتفاعه محال ، فإذا زالت الوحدة حصل التعدد وهو لا يتحقق إلا إذا تباين الشيئان بالهوية ، ثم ما به التباين إن كان من لوازم الماهية فكل شيئين فهما مختلفان بالماهية هذا خلف، وإن لم يكن من لوازمها كان الشيء حال عدمه موردا للصفات المتزايلة ، ولو جاز ذلك الجاز أن يكون محل الحركات والسكنات المتعاقبة عدما محضا وذلك عين السفسطة (٤)
For us [proof]: that the existence of blackness is identical with its being black, as has passed, so it is impossible for it to be black in the absence of existence. And because the blacknesses that are non-existent share in the realization (thubūt) opposed to pure negation and are differentiated by their specific particularities, and that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished, so the realization of those essences is additional to their specific quiddities, and they are, when we assume them devoid of the attribute of realization (1). And because the number of counted essences is susceptible to increase and decrease, so it is finite, and the opponent does not say so (2). And because the essences are eternal, so they cannot be caused, and the modes (wujūh) are states (ḥāl) according to them, so they cannot be caused according to them. And if neither the essence nor existence is effected by the agent, then the existing essence would be independent of the agent (3). And because the non-existent blackness is either one or many. If it is one, then if unity is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, its removal is impossible, so it must not become multiple in existence. And if it is not a necessary concomitant, then we suppose its removal, because for everything possible, supposing its removal is not impossible. So when unity is removed, multiplicity occurs, and it is not realized except when two things are distinct by identity. Then that by which they are distinct: if it is among the necessary concomitants of the quiddity, then any two things are different in quiddity — this is contrary [to the assumption]. And if it is not among its necessary concomitants, then the thing, in the state of its non-existence, would be a locus for successive attributes. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible that a pure non-existence be a locus for successive motions and rests, and that is pure sophistry (4).
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents four proofs that the possible non-existent is pure negation. First proof: existence is identical to essence (e.g., blackness), so a non-existent blackness cannot be black; the shared 'realization' among non-existent blacknesses is additional to their specific quiddities, so when assumed without that realization, they are nothing. Second proof: the number of non-existent essences can increase or decrease, implying finitude; but the opponent claims they are infinite? Actually, the opponent may not accept finitude. Third proof: if essences are eternal in non-existence, they cannot be caused by an agent; but the opponent may claim that the agent acts upon these essences to bring them into existence. If the agent does not cause either the essence or its existence, the existing essence would be independent of the agent. Fourth proof: a non-existent blackness is either one or many. If it is one and unity is necessary, it cannot become many in existence; if unity is not necessary, we can suppose its removal, leading to multiplicity. For two things to be distinct, there must be a differentiator. If that differentiator is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, then any two things would differ in quiddity, which is false. If it is not a necessary concomitant, then the thing in its state of non-existence would bear successive attributes, which is purely sophistical.
