التعدد
التباين
اللوازم
الصفات المتزايلة
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only works if we assume the non-existent is one, not many.
Correction: The proof starts with 'either one or many' and then shows that the 'one' branch leads to absurdity. The 'many' branch is implicitly rejected because if it were many, it would already be a thing, which is the point under dispute. The proof actually shows the impossibility of the 'one' branch, and by extension, the 'many' branch is also impossible because if it were many, each individual would have to be one, leading back to the same dilemma.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's fourth proof, what would follow if unity is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity of blackness?
A) Blackness can become many in existence
B) Blackness must remain one and cannot become many
C) Blackness can be a locus for attributes
D) Blackness would be pure non-existence

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth proof shows through a dilemma that the assumption of a non-existent blackness being a thing leads to logical contradictions or pure sophistry, confirming it is pure negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's proof forces us to acknowledge that a non-existent cannot be described with unity or multiplicity. When you hold onto a fantasy—a dream of a future that may never come, a grievance that no longer exists—are you not treating that non-existence as if it were real? What would it cost you to stop attributing existence to what is not there?
?
The proof shows that allowing even one attribute (like unity) in non-existence leads to infinite absurdities. In your own reasoning about matters of faith or life, where have you allowed one small contradiction thinking it would not lead to greater confusion? Will you trace that fault back to its root today?
?
Razi ends by equating the attribution of attributes to non-existence with 'pure sophistry'—a slavery to falsehood. Have you ever defended a belief that, when logically examined, rested on treating something imaginary as real? Are you willing to dismantle that deception now, even if it humbles you?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
The Opponents' First Proof: The Non-Existent Is Distinguishable
Razi presents the opponents' proof that the non-existent is distinguishable and therefore established.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the opponents' proof that the non-existent is established, including its two premises and the three aspects given to support the first premise.

Core Idea
The opponents argue that distinguishability implies reality, but this is contested.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the first of two proofs advanced by those who argue that the non-existent is a something. The proof rests on the claim that non-existents are distinguishable from each other, and since what is distinguishable must have some reality, the non-existent is established. Let us examine the argument and the evidence they provide for the key premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof structure
The proof has two premises and a conclusion. Find them in the text.
2
Examine the first premise
The first premise is 'the non-existent is distinguishable' (al-ma'dūm mutamayyiz). The opponents support this with three aspects, though only two are given here.
The first aspect uses knowledge; the second uses differential ability.
3
Consider the second premise
The second premise is 'everything distinguishable is established' (kull mutamayyiz thābit). This is a key kalam principle.
What does 'established' (thābit) mean here? It implies a kind of reality.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the non-existent is established. Ask yourself: does this proof succeed? Why might some reject it?
Razi's responses will challenge this conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا بأمرين. الحجة الأولى : المعدوم متميز وكل متميز ثابب فالمعدوم ثابت . بيان الأول من ثلاثة أوجه : أحدها: أن المعدوم معلوم وكل معلوم متميز ، أما إن المعدوم معلوم فلان طلوع الشمس غداً معلوم الآن وهو معدوم ، والحركة التي يمكنني أن أفعلها كالحركة إلى اليمين والشمال والتى لا يمكنني أن أفعلها كالطيران إلى السياء معلومة مع أنها معدومة ، وأما أن المعلوم متميز فلانى أميز بين الحركة التي أقدر عليها والتي لا أقدر عليها وأميز بين طلوع الشمس من مغربها ومن مشرقها وكذلك أحكم على إحدى الحركتين بأنها توجد غدا وعلى الأخرى بأنها لا توجد ، ولا معنى للتميز إلا ذلك ثانيها : أنى قادر على الحركة يمنة ويسرة وغير قادر على خلق السماء والأرض ، وهذا الامتياز حاصل قبل (۱) أقول : أما الحجة الأولى فقد من الكلام فيها . وأما الثانية فإلزام اشتراك الثبوت حالة العدم فهم . معترفون به وقوله « فهي حال فأمر صناعى معراة عن صفة الثبوت، جوابه أنا فرضناها معراة عن الوجوه لا عن النبوت ، ولا يقولون لما به الامتياز ثابتا بل إن كان ولابد فهي أحوال . (۲) أقول : إنهم يقولون الزيادة والنقصان يقتضيان التناهى في الموجودات لا في المعدومات . (۳) أقول : هم يقولون جعل الدوات موصوفة بالوجود أمر زائد عليها كالتركيب الذي هو يدل على الأجزاء وهو بالفاعل ، ولا يلزم من كون الأفراد غنية كون المركب غنيا عنه (٤) أقول : لهم أن يقولوا السواد حالة العدم لا يوصف بالكثرة وأيضاً إن كان معدوداً فالتباين ليس من لوازمها، ولا يجب أن يكون كل ما يكون لازماً للماهية زايلا فلا يكون المعدوم مورداً للصفات المتزايلة والسفسطة غير لازمة .
They argued with two proofs. The first proof: The non-existent is distinguishable, and everything distinguishable is established, so the non-existent is established. The demonstration of the first [premise] is from three aspects: First: that the non-existent is known, and everything known is distinguishable. As for that the non-existent is known, it is because sunrise tomorrow is known now while it is non-existent, and the movement that I can do like movement to the right and left and that which I cannot do like flying to the sky are known while they are non-existent. And as for that the known is distinguishable, it is because I distinguish between the movement I am able to do and that which I am not able to do, and I distinguish between sunrise from its west and from its east, and likewise I judge of one of the two movements that it will exist tomorrow and of the other that it will not exist; there is no meaning of distinguishability except that. Second: that I am able to move right and left and not able to create the heavens and the earth, and this distinction obtains before [existence?]. (1) I say: As for the first proof, the discussion of it has passed [؟]. And as for the second [proof], it is an imposition of the commonality of realization in the state of non-existence; they acknowledge it. And his statement 'they are a state, an artificial matter stripped of the attribute of realization': the answer is that we assume them stripped of the modes (wujūh) not of the realization, and they do not say that that by which distinction occurs is established; rather if it must be, then they are states (aḥwāl). (2) I say: They say that increase and decrease necessitate finitude in existents, not in non-existents. (3) I say: They say that making the essences described by existence is an additional matter upon them, like composition which indicates parts, and it is by the agent, and it does not follow from the individuals being independent that the composite is independent of Him. (4) I say: For them to say that blackness in the state of non-existence is not described with multiplicity, and also if it is counted, then distinction is not among its necessary concomitants, and it is not obligatory that everything that is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity be removed [؟], so the non-existent is not a locus for successive attributes, and sophistry is not necessary.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage contains the opponents' first proof (from two proofs) that the non-existent is established (thābit). The proof has two premises: the non-existent is distinguishable, and everything distinguishable is established. The first premise is supported by three aspects: (1) the non-existent is known (as future events and impossible actions are known), and everything known is distinguishable because we can differentiate between them. (2) We have different abilities regarding non-existent things (e.g., we can move right/left but not fly), and this distinction obtains even before existence. (3) The third aspect is not given in the text. Then Razi provides his responses in four editorial notes. In the first note, he says the first proof has already been discussed, and the second proof (of the opponents? actually the opponent's second proof is not shown) is that the commonality of realization in non-existence is acknowledged; the opponents' claim about 'states' is answered by noting that the essences are assumed stripped of modes, not of realization; and they do not claim that the differentiator is established. The second note: increase/decrease entail finitude only in existents, not in non-existents. Third note: making essences existent is an additional act of the agent, like composition; the independence of the parts does not entail independence of the whole from the agent. Fourth note: blackness in non-existence is not described by multiplicity; even if counted, distinction is not a necessary concomitant; so it does not follow that the non-existent is a locus for attributes, and the charge of sophistry is avoided.

Key Terms
المعدوم متميز
كل متميز ثابت
المعلوم
التميز
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The non-existent is completely unknowable and indistinguishable, so the opponents' argument is obviously false.
Correction: The opponents argue that the non-existent is known and distinguishable, which is a common experience. However, Razi will show that this does not prove it is 'established' in the sense they need.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of the opponents' first proof?
A) The non-existent is known
B) The non-existent is distinguishable
C) The non-existent is established
D) The non-existent is a thing

Key Takeaway
The opponents' proof attempts to establish the non-existent as something by arguing it is distinguishable, but the validity of this inference is open to debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a logical proof that tries to give the non-existent a kind of reality. Think of a future event you are anxious about—like an exam or a difficult conversation. Do you already treat that event as 'real' in your mind, causing you to feel its impact now? How does knowing that it is actually non-existent challenge you to change your mental relationship with it?
?
The opponents argued that because we can distinguish between possible and impossible actions, the non-existent must be something. In your daily life, when you distinguish between what you can and cannot do, do you sometimes use that as an excuse to avoid trusting Allah's decree? Where specifically can you replace self-reliance with reliance on Allah?
?
The first premise is supported by the claim that the non-existent is known. Consider your knowledge of Allah's attributes like His mercy or His anger. Is your knowledge of these real even though you cannot see them? How does this affect your worship today? Commit to one action that reflects that knowledge.
